  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_cfe805e0-81b0-4daf-9a79-e07095512b05'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initializeSiwa();

  <span id="freeText8273008677017650940" style="display:none">A writer in a totalitarian state is interrogated about the gruesome content of his short stories and their similarities to a number of child-murders that are happening in his town.<br /><br />'Sometimes you don't even know what you've been craving until the real thing comes along.' <i>New York Times</i><br /><br />'McDonagh is more than just a very clever theatrical stylist.   <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1112486904?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780822221" href="/book/show/17303062-the-pillowman">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1112486904" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18423858491113332440">The Pillowman: A Play, Martin McDonagh<br/><br/><b>The Pillowman</b> is a 2003 play by Irish playwright <b>Martin McDonagh</b>. It tells the tale of Katurian, a fiction writer living in a police state, who is interrogated about the gruesome content of his short stories and their similarities to a number of bizarre child murders occurring in his town. <br/><br/>Katurian, a writer of short stories that often depict violence against children, has been arrested by two detectives, Ariel and Tupolski, because some of his stories resem</span>
  <span id="freeText18423858491113332440" style="display:none">The Pillowman: A Play, Martin McDonagh<br /><br /><b>The Pillowman</b> is a 2003 play by Irish playwright <b>Martin McDonagh</b>. It tells the tale of Katurian, a fiction writer living in a police state, who is interrogated about the gruesome content of his short stories and their similarities to a number of bizarre child murders occurring in his town. <br /><br />Katurian, a writer of short stories that often depict violence against children, has been arrested by two detectives, Ariel and Tupolski, because some of his stories resemble recent child murders. When he hears that his brother Michal has confessed to the murders and implicated Katurian, he resigns himself to being executed but attempts to save his stories from destruction. <br /><br />The play contains both narrations and reenactments of several of Katurian's stories, including the autobiographical "The Writer and the Writer's Brother", which tells how Katurian developed his disturbed imagination by hearing the sounds of Michal being tortured by their parents.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز دوازدهم ماه جولای سال 2012 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: مرد بالشی - نمایشنامه؛ نویسنده: مارتین مکدونا؛ برگردان امیر امجد؛ تهران، نیلا، 1389، در 112ص، قلمرو هنر؛ شابک 9786001220715؛ موصوع نمایشنامه های نویسندگان انگلیسی - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: مرد بالشی - نمایشنامه؛ نویسنده: مارتین مکدونا؛ برگردان زهرا جواهری؛ تهران، افراز، 1393، در 157ص، مصور؛ از سری نمایشنامه های برتر جهان؛ شابک 9789642434480؛ <br /><br /><b>هشدار: </b> اگر میخواهید نمایشنامه را خود بخوانید، از خوانش ادامه ی این نگاره دوری کنید، رازها بر ملا میشوند. نمایش، مجموعه‌ ای از خرده‌ داستان‌های مینی‌مال با پایان دهشتناک است، که در پازلی رنگارنگ، حول خط اصلی نمایش، که داستان نویسنده ی همین مینیمال‌هاست، به زیبایی تنیده شده، و از خلال یک ماجرای جنایی، ذره‌ ذره، رازگشایی می‌شوند؛ نویسنده‌ ای به نام «کاتوریان»، داستان‌هایی درباره ی کودکانی می‌نویسد، که همگی به طرزی فجیع به قتل رسیده، یا آسیب می‌بینند، و الگوهای توصیفی او، توسط برادر کوچک‌ترش «مایکل»، که بلوغ عقلانی کمی دارد، به اجرا درآمده، و به مرگ چند کودک می‌انجامد؛ نمایش با بازجویی از «کاتوریان» آغاز، و با آمدن «مایکل»، و از خلال جلسات بازجویی، و رازگویی‌های دو برادر در زندان، پرده از راز مخوف زندگی این دو، و درعین‌ حال، دو بازجوی نمایش، برداشته شده، و در پایان نیز، با مرگ هر دو برادر به پایان می‌رسد؛ «مایکل» که ناتوان است، به دست برادر نویسنده‌ ی خویش به قتل می‌رسد، و «کاتوریان» نویسنده هم، به دست بازجوی خویش مرگ را میپذیرد؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="18423858491113332440" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1959695557" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1959695557" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1959695557?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/23230030-the-pillowman">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovers of dark comedy </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=mystery-crime-and-thrillers">mystery-crime-and-thrillers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=4-star-reads">4-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=darkness-horror-gothic">darkness-horror-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=plays">plays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1959695557" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9133564056026533261">Good theatre suspends you in reality. Good theatre captures a moment in time that could be now. And <i>The Pillowman</i> does this superbly. This is, indeed, a deeply disturbing play, chiefly because it could be real. <br/><br/>It all begins with some dark stories. A writer has written some brutal pieces about child murder and butchery. Someone has read his work and has decided to carry out the deeds within them. The writer, Katurian, has been brought in for questioning. The opening scene is reminiscent of Kafka</span>
  <span id="freeText9133564056026533261" style="display:none">Good theatre suspends you in reality. Good theatre captures a moment in time that could be now. And <i>The Pillowman</i> does this superbly. This is, indeed, a deeply disturbing play, chiefly because it could be real. <br><br>It all begins with some dark stories. A writer has written some brutal pieces about child murder and butchery. Someone has read his work and has decided to carry out the deeds within them. The writer, Katurian, has been brought in for questioning. The opening scene is reminiscent of Kafka’s <i> The Trial.</i> The protagonist is being investigated and put on trial for events he has no understanding of. Kafkaesque is a word that is on the tip of the performers tongue all through the scene, but it never is actually spoken despite the blatant allusions: it doesn’t quite need to be said. This effect is later removed as the situation becomes clearer.<br><br>What replaces it is a relationship straight out of <i> Of Mice and Men. </i> Katurian has a younger brother, one who is strikingly similar to Lenny. His concept of right and wrong is vague, though pure of heart; he will do anything he is told to do. The two brothers have a darker past, an abusive childhood that has bound them together out of survival and mutual affection. The investigators of the crime are certain it was one, or perhaps both, of them that carried out the killings. The questions begin as does the torture, though central to Katurian’s mind is what’s going to happen to his writing if he is found guilty. <br><br><i>“It isn't about being or not being dead, it's about what you leave behind”</i><br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491127463i/22377827._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>So the play questions the legacy of writing, and the responsibly of its content. Who is to blame in such a situation? Can the writer be held accountable for someone else’s obsessions and misconceptions over his work? This play may all sound terribly bleak, but running through it is a string of irony and self-reflexive moments. The characters draw attention to their own stupidity and the limitedness from the position in which they operate; thus, tragedy is infused with dark comedy making the play a true enjoyment to watch. <br><br>If you get the chance, I highly recommend watching a version of this after reading it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9133564056026533261" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer139390725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12539583511561779824"><blockquote>
  <i>That's a good story. That's something-esque. What kind of "esque" is it? I can't remember. I don't really go in for that "esque" sort of stuff anyway, but there's nothing wrong with that story. Is there?</i>
</blockquote><br/>I suspect that McDonagh would like you to think he is being Kafka-esque, but this is actually more King-esque. S. King with a thin veneer of literary affectation. Plus a dash of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/168668.Catch_22__Catch_22___1_' title='Catch-22 (Catch-22, #1) by Joseph Heller' rel='nofollow noopener'>Catch-22</a>.<br/><br/>That sounds harsh, but I actually found it moderately interest to read, and well done for what it was. If </span>
  <span id="freeText12539583511561779824" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>That's a good story. That's something-esque. What kind of "esque" is it? I can't remember. I don't really go in for that "esque" sort of stuff anyway, but there's nothing wrong with that story. Is there?</i>
</blockquote><br />I suspect that McDonagh would like you to think he is being Kafka-esque, but this is actually more King-esque. S. King with a thin veneer of literary affectation. Plus a dash of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/168668.Catch_22__Catch_22___1_" title="Catch-22 (Catch-22, #1) by Joseph Heller" rel="nofollow noopener">Catch-22</a>.<br /><br />That sounds harsh, but I actually found it moderately interest to read, and well done for what it was. If you don't mind all the child torture and police brutality.</span>

<div id="review_1760314635" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1760314635" />
    <a title="Hossein Sharifi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43579257-hossein-sharifi"><img alt="Hossein Sharifi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588060493p2/43579257.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1760314635?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hossein Sharifi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hossein Sharifi" href="/user/show/43579257-hossein-sharifi">Hossein Sharifi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780822221005" href="/book/show/336283.The_Pillowman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579257-hossein-sharifi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1760314635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15696861138270234702">Highly recommended <br/>.........................................................<br/>SPOILER ALERT ! <br/>.........................................................<br/><br/><b>Genre:</b><br/><br/>Grotesque or Dark Comedy ?!<br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475709238i/20754635._SY540_.jpg' width='220' height='300' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><b>What is the Grotesque?</b><br/>• The Grotesque fits in between the real and the fantastic (non-real). <br/>• The Grotesque simultaneously fits somewhere between being funny and being frightening. (This is a bit more difficult to gauge, as what is funny to one person is frightening to another, so maintaining a bit of an open mind i</span>
  <span id="freeText15696861138270234702" style="display:none">Highly recommended <br>.........................................................<br>SPOILER ALERT ! <br>.........................................................<br><br><b>Genre:</b><br><br>Grotesque or Dark Comedy ?!<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475709238i/20754635._SY540_.jpg" width="220" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>What is the Grotesque?</b><br>• The Grotesque fits in between the real and the fantastic (non-real). <br>• The Grotesque simultaneously fits somewhere between being funny and being frightening. (This is a bit more difficult to gauge, as what is funny to one person is frightening to another, so maintaining a bit of an open mind is helpful)<br>Furthermore, the Grotesque often contains a sort of fusion of human with animal, vegetable, machine, or some other combination. So, it could be a combination of a man and a dog, or a cat with a carrot, or a bird and a toad.<br>The simplest example of this that I can provide for you in literature is from the story The Metamorphosis by Franz Kafka, which essentially tells the story of a man who wakes up one day to discover that he has been somehow transformed into a person-sized insect.<br><b>What is Black / dark comedy? </b><br>Black comedy, also known as black humor or dark comedy, is a sub-genre of comedy and satire where topics and events that are usually treated seriously are treated in a satirical manner while still being portrayed as the negative events that they are. Typical targets are death, (mass) murder, suicide, blackmail, (domestic) violence, disease, insanity, handicaps, environmental disasters, famine, fear, child pornography/abuse, drug abuse, rape, castration, war, terrorism, racism, sexism, homophobia, bestiality and line-cutting.<br>Black Comedy doesn't necessarily have to involve death — anything tragic can be fodder for Black Comedy. A Kafka Komedy is a subtrope of Black Comedy in which the object of humor is abject failure.<br><b>Which one do u think it is ? a black comedy or a grotesque ????</b><br>by the way.. is grotesque a genre or is it just a literary feature ?!<br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475709238i/20754636._SY540_.jpg" width="200" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>To anyone unfamiliar with the story, The Pillowman might seem a misleading title. The Pillowman is one of Katurian's greatest stories, and is quite possibly the reason his brother decided to play God and toy with children's lives. Sure, it's not the main idea in the show, but it does play a very important part. The story is about a man, a Pillowman, whose purpose in life is to go to people who are suffering, and who have lived horrible lives, and comfort them. He then goes back in time and finds the people when they're still children, and he tells them that unless they end their lives now, they will lead terrible, and unhappy lives. His intentions are not malicious at all. In a way, he is saving these people. By convincing them to die while still so young, he is saving them from all those years of torment that he knows will drive them to suicide later on in life anyway.<br><br>Michal had lived an extremely traumatic life, having been tortured by his parents for seven years, and then watching them being murdered by his own brother. He suffered severe trauma and was left with a slight mental disability. Because of this, he is unable to properly process the stories that Katurian reads to him. When he hears The Pillowman, and hears about these children that are bound to lead horrible lives, he makes the assumption that ALL children are going to lead horrible lives and that he is doing them a favor by killing them before they really start to suffer.<br><br>The show takes place in a non-specific totalitarian state, which makes it impossible to determine geographical location, climate, or year. The characters are indoors, confined in a windowless cell which makes it impossible to tell even the time of day. Since we have no specific location, we have no way of knowing the economic, political, social, or religious environment. We get glimpses into Katurian’s past, yet they never mention a year or location.<br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475709238i/20754637._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br><b>Theme:</b><br><br>The main theme at the center of the dark, Anglo-Irish play The Pillowman is the effects of parental abuse.<br><br>As the topic that drives the plot forward, main character Katurian K. Katurian has to face the memories of his disturbed and abused childhood at the hands of his parents. The effect of such abuse resulted in his works as a story teller of macabre children's stories. As a result, he now faces the abusive interrogation from the police of a totalitarian establishment regarding the sudden macabre murders of children from the area.<br><br>Aside from Katurian, his brother Michal is also a victim of abuse by his and Katurian's parents. Likewise, he also undergoes investigation and also shows signs of psychological distress. What is shocking, however is that Michal is indeed guilty of the children's murders, leaving Katurian with no other choice but to kill his brother in what looks like a much more merciful way to die.<br><br>Faced with the possibility of having his body of work destroyed if he does not confess, Katurian takes the risk and blames himself for the murders. This leads to the second theme of the play, which is the defense and preservation of art even under the pressure of a suppressive system. Katurian prefers to basically get himself murdered rather than give up his stories. McDonagh places special emphasis on the importance of this theme, bringing with it equal significance to the overall atmosphere and style of the play.<br><br><br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475753500i/20761629.jpg" width="350" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Martin McDonagh seems to have drawn from the great Russian tradition of making his main characters utterly unlikeable, and tragically, comedically realistic.<br><br> <b>characters:</b><br><br>Katurian K. Katurian:<br>just a guy who writes horrific stories. Probably innocent of what the police are accusing him.<br>Katurian is a unique name but it closely resembles an Armenian last name, Khachaturian, which translates to “Cross-Bearer.” Katurian himself is a cross bearer because, though he may not survive the play, he has to live with the fact that his stories inspired the demise of three innocent children. Katurian was dragged into this despite his innocence in the matter.<br><br>At the beginning of the play, Katurian doesn’t know why he’s in custody. He believes that he is simply in there to help the police with their investigation and that he is in no way guilty of anything. At the end, Katurian feels lost and defeated. His brother confessed to killing innocent children and said it was because Katurian told him to, he realizes that there’s no escape for him. He knows that no matter what he does, he’s going to die, and the police are going to destroy all of his work.<br><br>Michal:<br>We don’t meet Michal until act 2, but at the beginning of act 2 he is being held in the jail cell next to the interrogation room where Katurian is being questioned. He knows that he killed those children, but doesn’t realize that he should feel guilty about it. Years of torture left him slow in the head, so he doesn’t understand the gravity of his actions, and feels no sympathy. His part ends with Katurian smothering him in his sleep, but by that time, he still doesn’t exactly feel bad about what he’s done. He explained to Katurian that he only killed the children to see how far-fetched his stories were, and never once apologized or expressed sympathy.<br><br>Detective Ariel :Claims to be "the bad cop" at the beginning, but transforms to the more "sympathetic" cop at the end. A totalitarian, dictorial cop and a counterpart to Tupolski. More a physical torturer than a verbal torturer.<br><br>Detective Tupolski: An investigator, less violent and aggressive, but very unfeeling. A counterpart to Detective Ariel, one of the totalitarian, dictitorial cops who dominate Katurian and Michal. Tupolski is "the good cop" at the beginning, but by being a good cop he spares no feelings. More verbal torturer than a physical torturer.<br><br>Martin McDonagh makes frequent use of this classic binary in his play – in fact, the entire play seems to depend on the contradiction of good and bad.<br>One of the first (and most basic) examples of this binary comes in the representation of the good cop/bad cop stereotype. We are immediately introduced to this duo and we immediately understand their roles in the play. Although these characters are not as deeply developed as Katurian and Michal, Tupolski and Ariel (good and bad, respectively) are no doubt important to the overall story.<br><br> Michal is at first seen as a good and innocent character: he cooperates with the police and seems incapable of the atrocities to which he eventually confesses. However, Michal must ultimately be considered bad – as are most serial child-killers. Again, through character development, McDonagh blurs the lines between good and bad and deconstructs the binary present throughout the play. <br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475753500i/20761630._SX540_.jpg" width="350" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br><br>References: 1. The Pillowman - Martin McDonagh<br>2. Study Guide for The Pillowman via gradesaver.com<br>3. The-pillowman via Enotes.com<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="15696861138270234702" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1454456558" />
    <a title="Liz Janet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5114433-liz-janet"><img alt="Liz Janet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574021381p2/5114433.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1454456558?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liz Janet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liz Janet" href="/user/show/5114433-liz-janet">Liz Janet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5114433-liz-janet?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1454456558" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4452109098790001945"><i>“It isn't about being or not being dead, it's about what you leave behind.”</i><br/><br/>This is not just a story of a writer in a totalitarian police state whose gruesome stories are happening. Nor is it about different forms of interrogation nor censorship. Nor is it solely on the responsibility of the author after his stories are being acted out in gruesome ways. This is a story of a writer becoming obsessed, while going interrogation, with having his stories survive his punishment. <br/><br/>The best part of the pl</span>
  <span id="freeText4452109098790001945" style="display:none"><i>“It isn't about being or not being dead, it's about what you leave behind.”</i><br><br>This is not just a story of a writer in a totalitarian police state whose gruesome stories are happening. Nor is it about different forms of interrogation nor censorship. Nor is it solely on the responsibility of the author after his stories are being acted out in gruesome ways. This is a story of a writer becoming obsessed, while going interrogation, with having his stories survive his punishment. <br><br>The best part of the play, to me at least, was the short stories by the writer told throughout the play. My favourite was "The Three Gibbet Crossroads," since I am still unable to see what could be worse than rape and murder within the context. Another brilliant one was "The Tale of the Town on the River," this is how re-tellings should be done all the time. "The Pillowman" makes me depressed, but I love it too much. And lastly "The Writer and the Writer's Brother," if this were a true story I would have cried even more than I did while reading it. <br><br><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2005/04/11/theater/reviews/a-storytelling-instinct-revels-in-horrors-fun.html?_r=0" rel="nofollow noopener">A Storytelling Instinct Revels in Horror's Fun</a><br><br>I wish I had read this play sooner, because it is brilliant, and now I wished I had seen the dramatization with David Tennant and Jim Broadbent. I will read this many times over, I guarantee it. <br>PLEASE READ IT! IT IS BRILLIANT AND GREAT AND JUST AWESOME!<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1429793525ra/14630758.gif" width="500" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br></span>

<div id="review_86297139" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/86297139" />
    <a title="unnarrator" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/323002-unnarrator"><img alt="unnarrator" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1214653984p2/323002.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/86297139?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="unnarrator" class="user" itemprop="url" name="unnarrator" href="/user/show/323002-unnarrator">unnarrator</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to unnarrator by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/89519-ab">AB</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer86297139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1722765417802925791">Maybe I'll sleep again <i>someday</i>...but seriously. Really, really, piercingly smart. funny, ghastly, and lovely. And ghastly. Did I mention ghastly? Ghastly in that Guy-de-Maupassant-rapes-Kafka kind of way.<br/><br/>(KATURIAN: 'That's a good story. That's something-esque. What kind of "esque" is it? I can't remember. I don't really go in for that "esque" sort of stuff anyway, but there's nothing wrong with that story. Is there?')<br/><br/>Should be four-and-a-half stars, really...and would have been five but for this</span>
  <span id="freeText1722765417802925791" style="display:none">Maybe I'll sleep again <i>someday</i>...but seriously. Really, really, piercingly smart. funny, ghastly, and lovely. And ghastly. Did I mention ghastly? Ghastly in that Guy-de-Maupassant-rapes-Kafka kind of way.<br /><br />(KATURIAN: 'That's a good story. That's something-esque. What kind of "esque" is it? I can't remember. I don't really go in for that "esque" sort of stuff anyway, but there's nothing wrong with that story. Is there?')<br /><br />Should be four-and-a-half stars, really...and would have been five but for this kind of Borgesian ripoff twist-finale which WORKS on the page (and has to be there, the whole play would collapse without it, it's a load-bearing ending), but I'm not sure how I would feel about its being <i>staged</i>. Isn't there a reason Borges only wrote short stories?<br /><br />Anyway, good for you Martin McDonagh. I bet your mother thinks you're a <i>right</i> freakshow, now; but the rest of us applaud you for not pulling your theater-of-cruelty punches, not one bit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1722765417802925791" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13272051049048700622">I was going to give this three out of five, since almost all of the events in the first two acts were disappointingly predictable; but the ending was brilliant, a great "twist", as Katurian would probably call it. Not only a twist in plot, but a twist in theme , in characterization, in the whole worldview of the story. <br/>PS. Oh,and I forgot to add: IT WAS THE SICKEST AND SIMULTANEOUSLY GENTLEST STORY I HAVE EVER READ. Is that even possible? Apparently, it is. </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer746682020" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4676079701004947735">This play is amazing. I just read some of the other reviews, and "devastating" is a word that also rings true. It is so horrific, and yet so funny, that the guilt you feel when you snigger is overwhelming. Again, reflecting another review, some plays don't read well - this one does. And yet, I can also visualise it on stage. But wonder whether some sort of age limit should be given! I was reading it with a view to assessing the proposal that our amateur dramatics group should put it on. While we</span>
  <span id="freeText4676079701004947735" style="display:none">This play is amazing. I just read some of the other reviews, and "devastating" is a word that also rings true. It is so horrific, and yet so funny, that the guilt you feel when you snigger is overwhelming. Again, reflecting another review, some plays don't read well - this one does. And yet, I can also visualise it on stage. But wonder whether some sort of age limit should be given! I was reading it with a view to assessing the proposal that our amateur dramatics group should put it on. While we undertake to put on "Plays of Merit", and don't say anything about taste, there is an element of impropriety about this play that makes me wonder if we'd get thrown out of the theatre. It's dark, very dark, and yet... surely this sort of play should be performed? It can't be ignored because it isn't very nice... or can it?<br /><br />The play is about a storyteller, and some of his stories, including the title story, are related over the play (although told, rather than acted out). It might seem odd, but I can imagine this working. <br /><br />This gets 5 stars for blowing my shoes off.<br /><br />**edit: our company did take this play forward. the audiences enjoyed it a lot, and the local church even gave a sermon ABOUT it, which was something else. (note: spoilers in Canon Black's sermon! <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://canonianblack.wordpress.com/2017/04/09/the-pillowman-and-the-passion/">https://canonianblack.wordpress.com/2...</a>) <br /></span>

<div id="review_2000341909" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2000341909" />
    <a title="Mia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23106940-mia"><img alt="Mia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600049623p2/23106940.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2000341909?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mia" href="/user/show/23106940-mia">Mia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=amorality-and-antiheroes">amorality-and-antiheroes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=i-cried">i-cried</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=dark-grim-scary">dark-grim-scary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=made-me-think">made-me-think</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2000341909" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10709249741357208952">Right, so there's a lot of places to go with this play, in terms reviewing it. Lots to work with. Because The Pillowman is about a lot of things—rage, childhood trauma, art, violence, stories, symbolism, the subconscious. But I think I've found something which ties all of those things together, so that's what I'm going to focus on here: surrogacy.<br/><br/>I'm not talking about having a baby for someone, I'm just using it in the general sense of the word—the state of being a surrogate. Being a stand-in fo</span>
  <span id="freeText10709249741357208952" style="display:none">Right, so there's a lot of places to go with this play, in terms reviewing it. Lots to work with. Because The Pillowman is about a lot of things—rage, childhood trauma, art, violence, stories, symbolism, the subconscious. But I think I've found something which ties all of those things together, so that's what I'm going to focus on here: surrogacy.<br /><br />I'm not talking about having a baby for someone, I'm just using it in the general sense of the word—the state of being a surrogate. Being a stand-in for someone else. As humans, more of our lives are spent making people surrogates than we'd like to think. How often do we take our anger out on the people who don't deserve it? How often do we take a liking (or a disliking) to someone because they remind us of someone else? How often do we (even subconsciously) make others the conduit for our pent-up pain over events in the past, over the fucked-up things our parents did and said or over the way we were bullied, how often do we take that simmering rage and, when it finally boils over, direct it not at the people who hurt us but at others quite unconnected with the original act?<br /><br />Quite often, I think.<br /><br />If this all sounds more than a bit Freudian to you, I understand. A lot of the concepts explored in this play are certainly of that nature. There's a lot of talk about childhoods and children, and all I could think about was how the act of killing a child is often imbued with such psychological and moral weight because of how the murderer sees his own self in the child, how the act is not, to him, a murder, but a mercy—how he sees himself and he destroys himself, and by doing so he tries to prevent himself from ever having existed at all because he so hates the knot of grief and rage and brokenness he was turned into because of his own childhood.<br /><br />Not that I'm pardoning child murderers. Although pardoning child murderers is a very real topic in The Pillowman, which is why I brought it up in the first place. So in case you haven't already guessed, you probably shouldn't read this play if you have any especial sensitivity regarding the death or torture of children. Well, I suppose every human person with a soul has this sensitivity, but I guess I just mean that you shouldn't read this if you can't handle that being a main component of the story. This isn't horror, so the intent isn't to frighten or repulse you, but these things certainly happen while Martin McDonagh is spinning a tale as terrible as this one.<br /><br />Writing is an important motif here, unsurprisingly since the protagonist is a writer and the plot concerns the implications of his stories. But here again we find surrogacy. What is writing but extended symbolism and self-exploration? Sounds masturbatory; probably is. Characters are not always surrogates for real people, sometimes they’re surrogates for ourselves, the parts of ourselves we can’t bear to analyse via classic introspection or therapy—the parts of ourselves who hate our parents, hate ourselves, desire obliteration more than anything—so we extract them and place them into little symbolic people made of words. It’s a dark take on the act of fiction writing and I wonder if Martin McDonagh believes it himself, or if Katurian Katurian is more of a nightmare, where the things we know and understand are horribly darkened and twisted up.<br /><br />And violence against surrogates even runs through the subtler details of The Pillowman. Consider the tale of the Little Green Pig and the fact that Katurian works for a butcher, something which is only mentioned once in the very first scene. Or the parents in The Little Jesus, how they compare to Katurian and Michal’s parents, and Ariel’s, and what became of all three sets.<br /><br />Every character in this play is guilty of surrogacy to some degree. The detectives who initially seem so boneheaded and brutish reveal their internal struggles and worldviews through some fantastic dialogue and monologues, and they both have a propensity to channel their anger and grief and unleash it on people who represent those that have hurt or abandoned them.<br /><br />And maybe the person who creates surrogates is a victim themselves, cycles of abuse and of psychological trauma without outlet. If this sounds unbearably depressing to you, that’s because it is, but the last scene of the play holds some hope in that regard, some light. Not much, little more than a pinprick or the flicker of a candle from a mile away, but it’s enough, I think. The world is dark, McDonagh tells you. So perhaps the candle is just there to emphasise the darkness surrounding it.<br /><br />I’m being vague about everything because you really should just read this play. (You can do so for free <a>here</a>.) It hits you like a punch in the gut—especially the titular story—but my god is it exceptional. Here’s a perfect balance between the cerebral and the concrete, a story that doesn’t forsake character development for symbolism and meta-fiction, which is philosophical and thought-provoking but also just a great tale. It seems perverse to say I loved The Pillowman, and if you read it you’ll understand why.<br /><br />But I did love it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10709249741357208952" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer39810995" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10654399600735746195">I don't know which theme was more exciting in this book: interrogation techniques of dictatorships, censorship, or the effects of literature on those who consume it. When I began reading this play I assumed the major theme would be the injustices suffered by the "writer" during his interrogation, and they were horrible. Then the writer become obsessed with the threat that his work will be destroyed. This theme is strong till the end of the play. Then there is the more controversial theme: are wr</span>
  <span id="freeText10654399600735746195" style="display:none">I don't know which theme was more exciting in this book: interrogation techniques of dictatorships, censorship, or the effects of literature on those who consume it. When I began reading this play I assumed the major theme would be the injustices suffered by the "writer" during his interrogation, and they were horrible. Then the writer become obsessed with the threat that his work will be destroyed. This theme is strong till the end of the play. Then there is the more controversial theme: are writers responsible for the actions of the people reading their works? How much responsibility does the intellectual creator have? This play addresses all these themes and more. It is dark, funny, and wonderful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10654399600735746195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_303351064" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/303351064" />
    <a title="Ben Loory" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/171197-ben-loory"><img alt="Ben Loory" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492548411p2/171197.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

  <span id="freeText3010238280140798944" style="display:none">3.5/5 Stars<br /><br />I had to read this play for class and I must say I was pleasantly surprised.   <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1501353364" />
    <a title="Gull" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43757020-gull"><img alt="Gull" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485199675p2/43757020.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1501353364?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gull" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gull" href="/user/show/43757020-gull">Gull</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43757020-gull?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43757020-gull?shelf=6-stars">6-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43757020-gull?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43757020-gull?shelf=drama">drama</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1501353364" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5353906500537614908">I absolutely <b>LOVE</b> this play! It is ridiculously amazing, and has earned its place up there amongst my all time favourites!<br/><br/><i>The Pillowman</i> is mind-blowingly dark, disturbing, and refreshingly original. It is both humorous and tragic. I think the humour comes from the condescension and irony, while it is tragic due to the central subject matter. The ending is shocking, in a strangely positive yet disturbing way. I had goose pimples throughout!<br/><br/>Katurian’s stories, largely involving the torture of chil</span>
  <span id="freeText5353906500537614908" style="display:none">I absolutely <b>LOVE</b> this play! It is ridiculously amazing, and has earned its place up there amongst my all time favourites!<br /><br /><i>The Pillowman</i> is mind-blowingly dark, disturbing, and refreshingly original. It is both humorous and tragic. I think the humour comes from the condescension and irony, while it is tragic due to the central subject matter. The ending is shocking, in a strangely positive yet disturbing way. I had goose pimples throughout!<br /><br />Katurian’s stories, largely involving the torture of children, inspire his brother to reproduce the horrors. Yet, as messed up as Katurian’s brother is, the final act of treating a mute girl pleasantly in accordance with one of Katurian’s stories, ‘The Little Green Pig’, illustrates that, perhaps, he was just <i>misguidedly</i> acting out the stories he read; this ultimately shows that literature—and by extension art—can stimulate people in both a positive way and a negative way.<br /><br /><i>The Pillowman</i> functions as a play dealing with totalitarianism, and <b>literary censure</b>, and so it is reminiscent of books like Bradbury’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4381.Fahrenheit_451" title="Fahrenheit 451 by Ray Bradbury" rel="nofollow noopener">Fahrenheit 451</a>.<br /><br />It is a definite must-read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5353906500537614908" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Amanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1139381-amanda"><img alt="Amanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1223343122p2/1139381.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/24555729?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda" href="/user/show/1139381-amanda">Amanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1139381-amanda?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1139381-amanda?shelf=school">school</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24555729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13187177588243527050">As an occasional theatre snob, I know that many plays are better seen than read. <i>The Pillowman</i> was not one of these, and had such an impact on me when I read it, that it stands alone as a piece of literature. (I hope to see it on stage soon) <br/><br/>I was so horrified and delighted by the events. The words toy with the reader's emotions, and made me feel terrible that I was enjoying it. The dark, twisted tales were varied, and well spaced throughout the play. My favorite being the sad tale of who the Pi</span>
  <span id="freeText13187177588243527050" style="display:none">As an occasional theatre snob, I know that many plays are better seen than read. <i>The Pillowman</i> was not one of these, and had such an impact on me when I read it, that it stands alone as a piece of literature. (I hope to see it on stage soon) <br /><br />I was so horrified and delighted by the events. The words toy with the reader's emotions, and made me feel terrible that I was enjoying it. The dark, twisted tales were varied, and well spaced throughout the play. My favorite being the sad tale of who the Pillowman was and what he did - not what I was expecting!<br /><br />The individual stories were far fetched, but just as they came true in the novel, it is terrifying to bring to mind all the real bizarre crimes we hear about. Despite differences, the world of the play is not far enough away from real life to make a reader comfortable. Perhaps it is this discomfort that give <i>The Pillowman</i> its power and attraction. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13187177588243527050" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_40982753" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40982753" />
    <a title="Jackie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1832157-jackie"><img alt="Jackie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436232829p2/1832157.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/40982753?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jackie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jackie" href="/user/show/1832157-jackie">Jackie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780822221005" href="/book/show/336283.The_Pillowman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1832157-jackie?shelf=irish-goodness-and-angst">irish-goodness-and-angst</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1832157-jackie?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1832157-jackie?shelf=mystery-suspense-and-noir">mystery-suspense-and-noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1832157-jackie?shelf=fantasy-scifi-and-other-oddities">fantasy-scifi-and-other-oddities</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40982753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3804188965999480882">Martin McDonagh makes me laugh at the most disturbing things. He really illuminates the degree of twistedness people posses. <br/>What makes this play so great is that it toys with our expectations. Is Tupolski <i>really</i> the good cop and is Ariel <i>really</i> the bad one? Is Michal a sympathetic character? Is the third victim even dead?<br/>This play is effing fantastic. I only wish I could have seen it on Broadway with Billy Crudup and Jeff Goldblum, or in London with David Tennant and Jim Broadbent. I'm convince</span>
  <span id="freeText3804188965999480882" style="display:none">Martin McDonagh makes me laugh at the most disturbing things. He really illuminates the degree of twistedness people posses. <br />What makes this play so great is that it toys with our expectations. Is Tupolski <i>really</i> the good cop and is Ariel <i>really</i> the bad one? Is Michal a sympathetic character? Is the third victim even dead?<br />This play is effing fantastic. I only wish I could have seen it on Broadway with Billy Crudup and Jeff Goldblum, or in London with David Tennant and Jim Broadbent. I'm convinced play scripts like to include the casts of the original production just to tease us with what might have been.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3804188965999480882" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Iris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1440073-iris"><img alt="Iris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604630344p2/1440073.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64197228?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Iris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Iris" href="/user/show/1440073-iris">Iris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780822221005" href="/book/show/336283.The_Pillowman">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Iris by: </span>
      my neighbor (who is perplexed by her fixation on this play)
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64197228" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16735542516010102314">This play - first performed in London in 2003 - is a devastating experiment. I would especially recommend it to reluctant readers. Though a veteran reader will pore over it in two or three sittings, the themes of the play sink in slowly and subtly. It encourages a reader to imagine alternate explanations for horrific events, and seek ways out of irrevocable situations. <br/><br/>To write more about "The Pillowman" is to give away surprises and twists in in the plot and the storytelling. Better to see for </span>
  <span id="freeText16735542516010102314" style="display:none">This play - first performed in London in 2003 - is a devastating experiment. I would especially recommend it to reluctant readers. Though a veteran reader will pore over it in two or three sittings, the themes of the play sink in slowly and subtly. It encourages a reader to imagine alternate explanations for horrific events, and seek ways out of irrevocable situations. <br /><br />To write more about "The Pillowman" is to give away surprises and twists in in the plot and the storytelling. Better to see for yourself the shifts in your understanding of the pillowman, the writer, his brother and interrogators.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16735542516010102314" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_463718992" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/463718992" />
    <a title="Kat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1008175-kat"><img alt="Kat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370231937p2/1008175.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/463718992?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kat" href="/user/show/1008175-kat">Kat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1008175-kat?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1008175-kat?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer463718992" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11222334485821051319">Basic Plot: Katurian wrote short stories that inspired several child murders, so now he's been taken by police in a totalitarian state who are attempting to discover the truth.<br/><br/>This was one intense play. The whole thing is essentially a police interrogation. We are never told where it is, though with the author being Irish there are some distinctly UK-type slang expressions used. It's not terribly specific, though, so it really could take place anywhere. The names are also varied in culture, so t</span>
  <span id="freeText11222334485821051319" style="display:none">Basic Plot: Katurian wrote short stories that inspired several child murders, so now he's been taken by police in a totalitarian state who are attempting to discover the truth.<br /><br />This was one intense play. The whole thing is essentially a police interrogation. We are never told where it is, though with the author being Irish there are some distinctly UK-type slang expressions used. It's not terribly specific, though, so it really could take place anywhere. The names are also varied in culture, so they don't make the place specific, either.<br /><br />The plot brings to mind a lot of themes associated with dystopian societies, particularly in the idea of who should be punished for what a storyteller writes. The writer, or the person who acts on the writing? The writer, or the person who misinterprets the meaning of the writing? There are a lot of questions in there. It's thought-provoking.<br /><br />I can see why the play won the Olivier award. The writing really does make the play, and I was able to vividly picture every character and scene as I read the dialogue. There is language and violence in it, so it's not exactly good fodder for a high school drama club, but some of the scenes would make great exercises for an acting class.<br /><br />Overall, an excellent play and an excellent read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11222334485821051319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9472244242349919319">To start with, this is funny. Really, really funny. Laugh out loud funny at parts.<br/><br/>It’s also a bit dark. Okay, more than a bit. Really dark. Pitch black dark. But funny. <br/><br/>Hopefully this play will make you a bit uncomfortable. It’s not pleasant, and it’s not an easy read. Not because it’s particularly convoluted, but due to the whole “pitch black” thing. You could describe it as “shocking” and not wander far from the truth.   <span id="freeText9472244242349919319" style="display:none">To start with, this is funny. Really, really funny. Laugh out loud funny at parts.<br /><br />It’s also a bit dark. Okay, more than a bit. Really dark. Pitch black dark. But funny. <br /><br />Hopefully this play will make you a bit uncomfortable. It’s not pleasant, and it’s not an easy read. Not because it’s particularly convoluted, but due to the whole “pitch black” thing. You could describe it as “shocking” and not wander far from the truth. Hopefully this play will make you a bit uncomfortable, because I’m not quite sure what to make of you if it doesn’t.<br /><br />But, remember, it’s funny. Really. Really. Funny.<br /></span>

<div id="review_846970061" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/846970061" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1018916-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216934475p2/1018916.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/846970061?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/1018916-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer846970061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8075582712520529971">I have heard talk of The Pillowman for years. Ok, not talk. Whispers. This play seems shrouded in mystery and myth. I have not seen the play performed, and after reading it, I'm not sure if I want to. The scenes are very intense, but also, there is quite a good bit of storytelling going on here, which is conducive to reading. I find it amazing that McDonagh not only conjured up this brilliant play, but also the brilliant dark fairy tales that are told within it. It is so effective though.<br/><br/>While r</span>
  <span id="freeText8075582712520529971" style="display:none">I have heard talk of The Pillowman for years. Ok, not talk. Whispers. This play seems shrouded in mystery and myth. I have not seen the play performed, and after reading it, I'm not sure if I want to. The scenes are very intense, but also, there is quite a good bit of storytelling going on here, which is conducive to reading. I find it amazing that McDonagh not only conjured up this brilliant play, but also the brilliant dark fairy tales that are told within it. It is so effective though.<br /><br />While reading the play, I couldn't help but see Kafka permeating the tone, setting, and characters. The interrogation style also reminded me of Pinter's The Birthday Party. Something sinister runs through the entire play, whether it is from a character, a story, or the world in which they live. The play takes place in a police station, either in an interrogation room or a cell. The main character, Katurian Katurian Katurian (echoes of The Trial's K) is detained by two police on suspicion of killing three little children, who all were killed in accordance with methods he details in three dark fairy tales he wrote. The rest of his work is now being investigated, and therefore his character along with it. He only ever wrote one "nice" story, and it is the one his brother, Michal, fixates on. Michal is brought in for questioning, as the police believe he will give up Katurian for the crimes. Michal has mental problems, and so make an easy target for the police - or so they think. <br /><br />There is so much misdirection in this play that McDonagh must have been a magician in a past life. While some of the subject matter is reprehensible and disturbing, he still finds a way to bring out the humanness in each of the characters. I was transfixed by the writing, the story and the characters that I read it in one sitting on one of the coldest days of the year. It sure made the time go by quickly. For anyone who thinks stage plays are boring or outdated should definitely read or see this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8075582712520529971" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_131715354" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/131715354" />
    <a title="Rick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/764994-rick"><img alt="Rick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289746453p2/764994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/131715354?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rick" href="/user/show/764994-rick">Rick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/764994-rick?shelf=drama">drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer131715354" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4979476171267245289">Two police detectives in a totalitarian state question two brothers, one a writer and one who is, as they say, “backward.” “A little slow,” counters his protective brother. The writer, perhaps in homage to Joseph Heller and his character Major Major Major, who when drafted in the air force is promoted to Major to avoid him being Airman Major Major or Lt. Major Major, has the same three names, Katurian Katurian Katurian. Katurian has no idea why he’s been arrested and is distressed when he discov</span>
  <span id="freeText4979476171267245289" style="display:none">Two police detectives in a totalitarian state question two brothers, one a writer and one who is, as they say, “backward.” “A little slow,” counters his protective brother. The writer, perhaps in homage to Joseph Heller and his character Major Major Major, who when drafted in the air force is promoted to Major to avoid him being Airman Major Major or Lt. Major Major, has the same three names, Katurian Katurian Katurian. Katurian has no idea why he’s been arrested and is distressed when he discovers his brother was also arrested. Based on the aggressive, at time violent questioning it seems to have something to do with the short stories Katurian writes, many of which feature children as murder victims. The play unfolds with layers of surprises and shifting understandings. Some of the surprises are anticipated but it doesn’t matter because surprise wasn’t the point. Despite thrilling moments, this is not a thriller or a comedy, despite some laugh out loud moments. It’s a complex drama of individuals, responsibility, and the tangled affects of circumstance. And it is also very much about storytelling, its importance, and, oddly, its risks. McDonagh has such a gift for plotting, character, dialogue that he amazes with how deftly he pulls off the absurd, the macabre, the compassionate. He is a literary magician and this is a stunning, darkly imaginative and profound drama.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4979476171267245289" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('NcIhNwGuquU+CwRDtIDW2l47suimAoT0CamHvfgkXBHzZwlXBc9yPoI+EipbCDngudBejcSyAPDITElbX3gGyw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LXFiSFcXKxWz4EMMeeDeJSzSq4ek+bJbKReICX/R7+Xr1EooU3bzzg/VVWWWaDEfyzlH4sZJNl/o8kbv2I21Pw==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_38700'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38700.Rabbit_Hole?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Rabbit Hole<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/21794.David_Lindsay_Abaire?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">David Lindsay-Abaire<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.06 avg rating &mdash; 7,054 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2006\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer7247979498609460913\">“David Lindsay-Abaire has crafted a drama that’s not just a departure but a revelation—an intensely emotional examination of grief, laced with wit, insightfulness, compassion and searing honesty.” –David Rooney, Variety“A beautifully observed new pla<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7247979498609460913\" style=\"display:none\">“David Lindsay-Abaire has crafted a drama that’s not just a departure but a revelation—an intensely emotional examination of grief, laced with wit, insightfulness, compassion and searing honesty.” –David Rooney, Variety“A beautifully observed new play blessed with David Lindsay-Abaire’s customary grace and wit.” –Ben Brantley, New York Times“With Rabbit Hole, David Lindsay-Abaire has crafted the most serious, simply told work of his career—a painstakingly beautiful, dramatically resourceful, exquisitely human new play.” –BackstageA story of loss, heartbreak and forgiveness—told through daily moments and emotional hurdles—as a family moves on after the accidental death of their 4-year-old. After a critically acclaimed Broadway premier and successful film adaptation (starring Nicole Kidman, Aaron Eckhart, and Diane West), Rabbit Hole has been hailed as an artistic breakthrough for the highly regarded Lindsay-Abaire. A drama of what comes after tragedy, it captures “the awkwardness and pain of thinking people faced with an unthinkable situation—and eventually, their capacity for survival.” -USA TodayDavid Lindsay-Abaire is the Pulitzer-winning author of Rabbit Hole, which was made into a feature film. He is the author of Good People, Fuddy Meers, Wonder of the World, A Devil Inside and Kimberly Akimbo, as well as the book and lyrics to Shrek the Musical. He has written the screenplays for Rabbit Hole, Rise of the Guardians and Oz: The Great and Powerful. His death has brought into her midst both her sister, Claire, who wants to take Catherine back to New York with her, and Hal, a former student of Catherine\'s father who hopes to find some hint of Robert\'s genius among his incoherent scribblings. The passion that Hal feels for math both moves and angers Catherine, who, in her exhaustion, is torn between missing her father and resenting the great sacrifices she made for him. For Catherine has inherited at least a part of her father\'s brilliance -- and perhaps some of his instability as well. As she and Hal become attracted to each other, they push at the edges of each other\'s knowledge, considering not only the unpredictability of genius but also the human instinct toward love and That made it<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1145144378868987364\" style=\"display:none\">It had occurred to her early that in her position - that of a young person spending, in framed and wired confinement, the life of a guinea-pig or a magpie - she should know a great many persons without their recognising the acquaintance. That made it an emotion the more lively - though singularly rare and always, even then, with opportunity still very much smothered - to see any one come in whom she knew outside, as she called it, any one who could add anything to the meanness of her function. Her function was to sit there with two young men - the other telegraphist and the counter-clerk; to mind the &quot;sounder,&quot; which was always going, to dole out stamps and postal-orders, weigh letters, answer stupid questions, give difficult change and, more than anything else, count words as numberless as the sands of the sea, the words of the telegrams thrust, from morning to night, through the gap left in the high lattice, across the encumbered shelf that her forearm ached with rubbing. This transparent screen fenced out or fenced in, according to the side of the narrow counter on which the human lot was cast, the duskiest corner of a shop pervaded not a little, in winter, by the poison of perpetual gas, and at all times by the presence of hams, cheese, dried fish, soap, varnish, paraffin and other solids and fluids that she came to know perfectly by their smells without       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/77295.Martin_McDonagh">About Martin McDonagh</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

</a></div>

followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8016985847444755052">While still in his twenties, the Anglo-Irish playwright Martin McDonagh filled houses in New York and London, was showered with the theatre world's most prestigious accolades, and electrified audiences with his cunningly crafted and outrageous tragicomedies.</span>

</div>
